<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_military_personnel">On military personnel</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Military_personnel" title="Military personnel">Military personnel</a> subject to combat in war often suffer mental and physical injuries, including depression, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">posttraumatic stress disorder</a>, disease, injury, and death.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In every war in which American soldiers have fought in, the chances of becoming a psychiatric casualty â of being debilitated for some period of time as a consequence of the stresses of military life â were greater than the chances of being killed by enemy fire.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><i>No More Heroes</i>, Richard Gabriel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-War_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-War-20">[19]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Swank and Marchand's World War II study found that after sixty days of continuous combat, 98% of all surviving military personnel will become psychiatric casualties. Psychiatric casualties manifest themselves in fatigue cases, confusional states, conversion hysteria, anxiety, obsessional and compulsive states, and character disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1996_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1996-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>One-tenth of mobilised American men were hospitalised for mental disturbances between 1942 and 1945, and after thirty-five days of uninterrupted combat, 98% of them manifested psychiatric disturbances in varying degrees.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><i>14â18: Understanding the Great War</i>, StÃ©phane Audoin-Rouzeau, Annette Becker<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-War_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-War-20">[19]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2064" data-file-width="3172" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG/200px-1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG/300px-1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG/400px-1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1871_Vereshchagin_Apotheose_des_Krieges_anagoria.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Apotheosis_of_War" title="The Apotheosis of War">The Apotheosis of War</a></i> (1871) by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Vereshchagin" title="Vasily Vereshchagin">Vasily Vereshchagin</a></div></div></div>
<p>Additionally, it has been estimated anywhere from 18% to 54% of Vietnam war veterans suffered from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">posttraumatic stress disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1996_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1996-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based on 1860 census figures, 8% of all white American males aged 13 to 43 died in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, including about 6% in the North and approximately 18% in the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> The war remains the deadliest conflict in American history, resulting in the deaths of 620,000 military personnel. <a href="/wiki/United_States_military_casualties_of_war" title="United States military casualties of war">United States military casualties of war</a> since 1775 have totaled over two million. Of the 60Â million European military personnel who were mobilized in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, 8Â million were killed, 7Â million were permanently disabled, and 15Â million were seriously injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg/220px-DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg/330px-DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg/440px-DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The remains of dead <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crow_Indians" title="Crow Indians">Crow Indians</a> killed and scalped by Sioux c. 1874</div></div></div>
<p>During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>'s retreat from Moscow, more French military personnel died of <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> than were killed by the Russians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Of the 450,000 soldiers who crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neman_River" title="Neman River">Neman</a> on 25 June 1812, less than 40,000 returned. More military personnel were killed from 1500 to 1914 by typhus than from military action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIME_Magazine_1940_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIME_Magazine_1940-65">[64]</a></sup> In addition, if it were not for modern medical advances there would be thousands more dead from disease and infection. For instance, during the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> reported it conscripted 184,899 sailors, of whom 133,708 (72%) died of disease or were 'missing'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is estimated that between 1985 and 1994, 378,000 people per year died due to war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_civilians">On civilians</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties" title="Civilian casualties">Civilian casualties</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2379" data-file-width="5446" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg/220px-Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg/330px-Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg/440px-Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hanging_from_The_Miseries_and_Misfortunes_of_War_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Les_Grandes_Mis%C3%A8res_de_la_guerre" title="Les Grandes MisÃ¨res de la guerre">Les Grandes MisÃ¨res de la guerre</a></i> depict the destruction unleashed on civilians during the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Most wars have resulted in significant loss of life, along with destruction of infrastructure and resources (which may lead to <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, disease, and death in the <a href="/wiki/Civilian" title="Civilian">civilian</a> <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a>). During the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> in Europe, the population of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> was reduced by 15 to 40 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> Civilians in war zones may also be subject to war atrocities such as <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>, while survivors may suffer the psychological aftereffects of witnessing the destruction of war. War also results in lower quality of life and worse health outcomes. A medium-sized conflict with about 2,500 battle deaths reduces civilian life expectancy by one year and increases <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> by 10% and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> by 3.3%. Additionally, about 1.8% of the population loses access to <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most estimates of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties" title="World War II casualties">World War II casualties</a> indicate around 60Â million people died, 40Â million of whom were civilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Deaths in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> were around <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="World War II casualties of the Soviet Union">27 million</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Since a high proportion of those killed were young men who had not yet fathered any children, population growth in the postwar Soviet Union was much lower than it otherwise would have been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Military_Keynesianism" title="Military Keynesianism">Military Keynesianism</a></div>
<p>Once a war has ended, losing nations are sometimes required to pay <a href="/wiki/War_reparations" title="War reparations">war reparations</a> to the victorious nations. In certain cases, land is ceded to the victorious nations. For example, the territory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a> has been traded between France and Germany on three different occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Typically, war becomes intertwined with the economy and many wars are partially or entirely based on economic reasons. Some economists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe war can stimulate a country's economy (high government spending for World War II is often credited with bringing the U.S. out of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> by most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keynesian" title="Keynesian">Keynesian</a> economists), but in many cases, such as the wars of Louis XIV, the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, warfare primarily results in damage to the economy of the countries involved. For example, Russia's involvement in World War I took such a toll on the Russian economy that it almost collapsed and greatly contributed to the start of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution of 1917</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyprian_War.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="589" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cyprian_War.jpg/170px-Cyprian_War.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cyprian_War.jpg/255px-Cyprian_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cyprian_War.jpg/340px-Cyprian_War.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyprian_War.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>'s Napoleon Square in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> was the most financially costly conflict in history; its belligerents cumulatively spent about a trillion U.S. dollars on the <a href="/wiki/War_effort" title="War effort">war effort</a> (as adjusted to 1940 prices).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s ended as nations increased their production of war materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the war, 70% of European industrial infrastructure was destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> Property damage in the Soviet Union inflicted by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Axis invasion</a> was estimated at a value of 679Â billion rubles. The combined damage consisted of complete or partial destruction of 1,710 cities and towns, 70,000 villages/hamlets, 2,508 church buildings, 31,850 industrial establishments, 40,000Â mi (64,374Â km) of railroad, 4100 railroad stations, 40,000 hospitals, 84,000 schools, and 43,000 public libraries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_motivation">Theories of motivation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/International_relations_theory" title="International relations theory">International relations theory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Szigetvar_1566.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1819" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Szigetvar_1566.jpg/170px-Szigetvar_1566.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Szigetvar_1566.jpg/255px-Szigetvar_1566.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Szigetvar_1566.jpg/340px-Szigetvar_1566.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Szigetvar_1566.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_campaigns_of_Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="List of campaigns of Suleiman the Magnificent">Ottoman campaign</a> for territorial <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">expansion in Europe</a> in 1566</div></div></div>
<p>There are many theories about the motivations for war, but no consensus about which are most common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz" title="Carl von Clausewitz">Carl von Clausewitz</a> said, 'Every age has its own kind of war, its own limiting conditions, and its own peculiar preconceptions.'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychoanalytic">Psychoanalytic</span></h3>
<p>Dutch <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychoanalyst" title="Psychoanalyst">psychoanalyst</a> <a href="/wiki/Joost_Meerloo" title="Joost Meerloo">Joost Meerloo</a> held that, "War is often...a mass discharge of accumulated internal rage (where)...the inner fears of mankind are discharged in mass destruction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meerloo_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-meerloo-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other psychoanalysts such as E.F.M. Durban and <a href="/wiki/John_Bowlby" title="John Bowlby">John Bowlby</a> have argued human beings are <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inherently</a> violent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> This aggressiveness is fueled by <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(psychology)" title="Displacement (psychology)">displacement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_projection" title="Psychological projection">projection</a> where a person transfers his or her grievances into bias and hatred against other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(human_classification)" title="Race (human classification)">races</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideologies</a>. By this theory, the nation state preserves order in the local society while creating an outlet for aggression through warfare.
</p><p>The Italian psychoanalyst <a href="/wiki/Franco_Fornari" title="Franco Fornari">Franco Fornari</a>, a follower of <a href="/wiki/Melanie_Klein" title="Melanie Klein">Melanie Klein</a>, thought war was the paranoid or projective "elaboration" of mourning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Fornari thought war and violence develop out of our "love need": our wish to preserve and defend the sacred object to which we are attached, namely our early mother and our fusion with her. For the adult, nations are the sacred objects that generate warfare. Fornari focused upon sacrifice as the essence of war: the astonishing willingness of human beings to die for their country, to give over their bodies to their nation.
</p><p>Despite Fornari's theory that man's altruistic desire for self-sacrifice for a noble cause is a contributing factor towards war, few wars have originated from a desire for war among the general populace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> Far more often the general population has been reluctantly drawn into war by its rulers. One psychological theory that looks at the leaders is advanced by Maurice Walsh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> He argues the general populace is more neutral towards war and wars occur when leaders with a psychologically abnormal disregard for human life are placed into power. War is caused by leaders who seek war such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a>. Such leaders most often come to power in times of crisis when the populace opts for a decisive leader, who then leads the nation to war.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Naturally, the common people don't want war; neither in Russia nor in England nor in America, nor for that matter in Germany. That is understood. But, after all, it is the leaders of the country who determine the policy and it is always a simple matter to drag the people along, whether it is a democracy or a fascist dictatorship or a Parliament or a Communist dictatorship. ... the people can always be brought to the bidding of the leaders. That is easy. All you have to do is tell them they are being attacked and denounce the pacifists for lack of patriotism and exposing the country to danger. It works the same way in any country.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite><a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann GÃ¶ring">Hermann GÃ¶ring</a> at the Nuremberg trials, 18 April 1946<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary">Evolutionary</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_warfare" title="Prehistoric warfare">Prehistoric warfare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1827" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg/220px-Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg/330px-Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg/440px-Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Women and priests retrieve the dead bodies of Swabian soldiers just outside the city gates of Constance after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Schwaderloh" title="Battle of Schwaderloh">battle of Schwaderloh</a>. <i>(<a href="/wiki/Luzerner_Schilling" title="Luzerner Schilling">Luzerner Schilling</a>)</i></div></div></div>
<p>Several theories concern the evolutionary origins of warfare. There are two main schools: One sees organized warfare as emerging in or after the Mesolithic as a result of complex social organization and greater population density and <a href="/wiki/Competition" title="Competition">competition</a> over resources; the other sees human warfare as a more ancient practice derived from common animal tendencies, such as territoriality and sexual competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The latter school argues that since warlike behavior patterns are found in many primate species such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ApesWar_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-ApesWar-90">[89]</a></sup> as well as in many <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ant</a> species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AntsLandmines_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-AntsLandmines-91">[90]</a></sup> group conflict may be a general feature of animal social behavior. Some proponents of the idea argue that war, while innate, has been intensified greatly by developments of technology and social organization such as weaponry and states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Psychologist and linguist <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a> argued that war-related behaviors may have been naturally selected in the ancestral environment due to the benefits of victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (July 2016)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>  He also argued that in order to have credible <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deterrence</a> against other groups (as well as on an individual level), it was important to have a reputation for retaliation, causing humans to develop instincts for <a href="/wiki/Revenge" title="Revenge">revenge</a> as well as for protecting a group's (or an individual's) reputation ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honor" title="Honor">honor</a>").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_(panorama).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4240" data-file-width="8763" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG/260px-Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG/390px-Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG/520px-Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_(panorama).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Increasing population and constant warfare among the <a href="/wiki/Maya_warfare" title="Maya warfare">Maya</a> city-states over resources may have contributed to the eventual <a href="/wiki/Classic_Maya_collapse" title="Classic Maya collapse">collapse</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a> by AD 900.</div></div></div>
<p>Crofoot and Wrangham have argued that warfare, if defined as group interactions in which "coalitions attempt to aggressively dominate or kill members of other groups", is a characteristic of most human societies. Those in which it has been lacking "tend to be societies that were politically dominated by their neighbors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HumanPrimateAggression_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-HumanPrimateAggression-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ashley_Montagu" title="Ashley Montagu">Ashley Montagu</a> strongly denied universalistic instinctual arguments, arguing that social factors and childhood socialization are important in determining the nature and presence of warfare. Thus, he argues, warfare is not a universal human occurrence and appears to have been a historical invention, associated with certain types of human societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> Montagu's argument is supported by ethnographic research conducted in societies where the concept of aggression seems to be entirely absent, e.g. the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chewong" title="Chewong">Chewong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semai_people" title="Semai people">Semai</a> of the Malay peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> Bobbi S. Low has observed correlation between warfare and education, noting societies where warfare is commonplace encourage their children to be more aggressive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_2">Economic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A,_T-55,_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1890" data-file-width="2860" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg/220px-Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg/330px-Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg/440px-Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A,_T-55,_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kuwaiti <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">oil wells</a> on fire, during the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, 1 March 1991</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Resource_war" title="Resource war">Resource war</a></div>
<p>War can be seen as a growth of economic competition in a competitive international system. In this view wars begin as a pursuit of markets for <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> and for wealth. War has also been linked to <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> by economic historians and development economists studying <a href="/wiki/State-building" title="State-building">state-building</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_capacity" title="Fiscal capacity">fiscal capacity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> While this theory has been applied to many conflicts, such counter arguments become less valid as the increasing mobility of capital and information level the distributions of wealth worldwide, or when considering that it is relative, not absolute, wealth differences that may fuel wars. There are those on the extreme <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_(politics)" title="Right (politics)">right</a> of the political spectrum who provide support, fascists in particular, by asserting a natural right of a strong nation to whatever the weak cannot hold by force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> Some centrist, capitalist, world leaders, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Presidents of the United States">Presidents of the United States</a> and U.S. <a href="/wiki/General_officer" title="General officer">Generals</a>, expressed support for an economic view of war.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marxist">Marxist</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marxist_explanations_of_warfare" title="Marxist explanations of warfare">Marxist explanations of warfare</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> theory of war is quasi-economic in that it states all modern wars are caused by competition for resources and markets between great (<a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialist</a>) powers, claiming these wars are a natural result of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>. Marxist economists <a href="/wiki/Karl_Kautsky" title="Karl Kautsky">Karl Kautsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosa_Luxemburg" title="Rosa Luxemburg">Rosa Luxemburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hilferding" title="Rudolf Hilferding">Rudolf Hilferding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> theorized that <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> was the result of capitalist countries needing new <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">markets</a>. Expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a> is only possible if there is a corresponding growth in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consumer_demand" title="Consumer demand">consumer demand</a>. Since the workers in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capitalist_economy" title="Capitalist economy">capitalist economy</a> would be unable to fill the demand, producers must expand into non-capitalist markets to find consumers for their goods, hence driving imperialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic">Demographic</span></h3>
<p>Demographic theories can be grouped into two classes, Malthusian and youth bulge theories:
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malthusian">Malthusian</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1006" data-file-width="1517" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG/220px-Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG/330px-Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG/440px-Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Marine helicopter on patrol in Somalia as part of the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Task_Force" title="Unified Task Force">Unified Task Force</a>, 1992</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian theories</a> see expanding population and scarce resources as a source of violent conflict.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> in 1095, on the eve of the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>, advocating Crusade as a solution to European overpopulation, said:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For this land which you now inhabit, shut in on all sides by the sea and the mountain peaks, is too narrow for your large population; it scarcely furnishes food enough for its cultivators. Hence it is that you murder and devour one another, that you wage wars, and that many among you perish in civil strife. Let hatred, therefore, depart from among you; let your quarrels end. Enter upon the road to the Holy Sepulchre; wrest that land from a wicked race, and subject it to yourselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>This is one of the earliest expressions of what has come to be called the Malthusian theory of war, in which wars are caused by expanding populations and limited resources. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> (1766â1834) wrote that populations always increase until they are limited by war, disease, or <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>The violent <a href="/wiki/Herder%E2%80%93farmer_conflicts_in_Nigeria" title="Herderâfarmer conflicts in Nigeria">herderâfarmer conflicts in Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudanese_nomadic_conflicts" title="Sudanese nomadic conflicts">Sudan</a> and other countries in the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> region have been exacerbated by <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a> and population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Youth_bulge">Youth bulge</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2017_world_map,_median_age_by_country.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/2017_world_map%2C_median_age_by_country.svg/310px-2017_world_map%2C_median_age_by_country.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/2017_world_map%2C_median_age_by_country.svg/465px-2017_world_map%2C_median_age_by_country.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/2017_world_map%2C_median_age_by_country.svg/620px-2017_world_map%2C_median_age_by_country.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2017_world_map,_median_age_by_country.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Population_pyramid" title="Population pyramid">Median age</a> by country. War reduces life expectancy. A youth bulge is evident for <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Africa" title="Demographics of Africa">Africa</a>, and to a lesser extent in some countries in West Asia, South Asia, Southeast Asia and Central America.</div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Heinsohn" title="Gunnar Heinsohn">Heinsohn</a>, who proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Youth_bulge" title="Youth bulge">youth bulge</a> theory in its most generalized form, a youth bulge occurs when 30 to 40 percent of the males of a nation belong to the "fighting age" cohorts from 15 to 29 years of age. It will follow periods with <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rates</a> as high as 4â8 children per woman with a 15â29-year delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Heinsohn saw both past "Christianist" European colonialism and imperialism, as well as today's Islamist civil unrest and terrorism as results of high birth rates producing youth bulges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Among prominent historical events that have been attributed to youth bulges are the role played by the historically large youth cohorts in the rebellion and revolution waves of early modern Europe, including the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> of 1789,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> and the effect of economic depression upon the largest German youth cohorts ever in explaining the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> in Germany in the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> The 1994 <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a> has also been analyzed as following a massive youth bulge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Youth bulge theory has been subjected to statistical analysis by the World Bank,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Population_Action_International" title="Population Action International">Population Action International</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Institute_for_Population_and_Development" title="Berlin Institute for Population and Development">Berlin Institute for Population and Development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Youth bulge theories have been criticized as leading to racial, gender and age discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural">Cultural</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Parker_(historian)" title="Geoffrey Parker (historian)">Geoffrey Parker</a> argues that what distinguishes the "Western way of war" based in Western Europe chiefly allows historians to explain its extraordinary success in conquering most of the world after 1500:</p><blockquote><p> The Western way of war rests upon five principal foundations: technology, discipline, a highly aggressive military tradition, a remarkable capacity to innovate and to respond rapidly to the innovation of others andâfrom about 1500 onwardâa unique system of war finance. The combination of all five provided a formula for military success....The outcome of wars has been determined less by technology, then by better war plans, the achievement of surprise, greater economic strength, and above all superior discipline. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Parker argues that Western armies were stronger because they emphasized discipline, that is, "the ability of a formation to stand fast in the face of the enemy, where they're attacking or being attacked, without giving way to the natural impulse of fear and panic."  Discipline came from drills and marching in formation, target practice, and creating small "artificial kinship groups: such as the company and the platoon, to enhance psychological cohesion and combat efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationalist">Rationalist</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg/220px-LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg/330px-LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg/440px-LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. soldiers directing artillery on enemy trucks in A Shau Valley, April 1968</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rationalism_(international_relations)" title="Rationalism (international relations)">Rationalism</a> is an <a href="/wiki/International_relations_theory" title="International relations theory">international relations theory</a> or framework. Rationalism (and <a href="/wiki/Neorealism_(international_relations)" title="Neorealism (international relations)">Neorealism (international relations)</a>) operate under the assumption that states or international actors are rational, seek the best possible outcomes for themselves, and desire to avoid the costs of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-119">[118]</a></sup> Under one <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">game theory</a> approach, rationalist theories posit all actors can bargain, would be better off if war did not occur, and likewise seek to understand why war nonetheless reoccurs. Under another rationalist game theory without bargaining, the <a href="/wiki/Peace_war_game" title="Peace war game">peace war game</a>, optimal strategies can still be found that depend upon number of iterations played. In "Rationalist Explanations for War", <a href="/wiki/James_Fearon" title="James Fearon">James Fearon</a> examined three rationalist explanations for why some countries engage in war:
</p>
<ul><li>Issue indivisibilities</li>
<li>Incentives to misrepresent or <a href="/wiki/Information_asymmetry" title="Information asymmetry">information asymmetry</a></li>
<li>Commitment problems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-119">[118]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>"Issue indivisibility" occurs when the two parties cannot avoid war by bargaining, because the thing over which they are fighting cannot be shared between them, but only owned entirely by one side or the other.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_(Vietnam_War)_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="673" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg/220px-U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg/330px-U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg/440px-U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_(Vietnam_War)_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Marines direct a concentration of fire at their opponents, Vietnam, 8 May 1968</div></div></div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Information_asymmetry" title="Information asymmetry">Information asymmetry</a> with incentives to misrepresent" occurs when two countries have secrets about their individual capabilities, and do not agree on either: who would win a war between them, or the magnitude of state's victory or loss. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Blainey" title="Geoffrey Blainey">Geoffrey Blainey</a> argues that war is a result of miscalculation of strength. He cites historical examples of war and demonstrates, "war is usually the outcome of a diplomatic crisis which cannot be solved because both sides have conflicting estimates of their bargaining power."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> Thirdly, bargaining may fail due to the states' inability to make credible commitments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the rationalist tradition, some theorists have suggested that individuals engaged in war suffer a normal level of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_bias" title="Cognitive bias">cognitive bias</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> but are still "as rational as you and me".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> According to philosopher <a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a>, "Most instigators of conflict overrate their chances of success, while most participants underrate their chances of injury...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> King asserts that "Most catastrophic military decisions are rooted in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GroupThink" title="GroupThink">GroupThink</a>" which is faulty, but still rational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rationalist theory focused around bargaining is currently under debate. The Iraq War proved to be an anomaly that undercuts the validity of applying rationalist theory to some wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_science">Political science</span></h3>
<p>The statistical analysis of war was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Fry_Richardson" title="Lewis Fry Richardson">Lewis Fry Richardson</a> following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. More recent databases of wars and armed conflict have been assembled by the <a href="/wiki/Correlates_of_War" title="Correlates of War">Correlates of War</a> Project, Peter Brecke and the <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_Conflict_Data_Program" title="Uppsala Conflict Data Program">Uppsala Conflict Data Program</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The following subsections consider causes of war from system, societal, and individual levels of analysis. This kind of division was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Waltz" title="Kenneth Waltz">Kenneth Waltz</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Man,_the_State,_and_War" title="Man, the State, and War">Man, the State, and War</a></i> and has been often used by political scientists since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 143">:â143â</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="System-level">System-level</span></h4>
<p>There are several different <a href="/wiki/International_relations_theory" title="International relations theory">international relations theory</a> schools. Supporters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Realism_in_international_relations" title="Realism in international relations">realism in international relations</a> argue that the motivation of states is the quest for security, and conflicts can arise from the inability to distinguish defense from offense, which is called the <a href="/wiki/Security_dilemma" title="Security dilemma">security dilemma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 145">:â145â</span></sup>
</p><p>Within the realist school as represented by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Morgenthau" title="Hans Morgenthau">Hans Morgenthau</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Neorealism_(international_relations)" title="Neorealism (international relations)">neorealist</a> school represented by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Waltz" title="Kenneth Waltz">Kenneth Waltz</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Mearsheimer" title="John Mearsheimer">John Mearsheimer</a>, two main sub-theories are:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(international_relations)" title="Balance of power (international relations)">Balance of power</a> theory: States have the goal of preventing a single state from becoming a hegemon, and war is the result of the would-be hegemon's persistent attempts at power acquisition. In this view, an international system with more equal distribution of power is more stable, and "movements toward unipolarity are destabilizing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 147">:â147â</span></sup> However, evidence has shown power <a href="/wiki/Polarity_(international_relations)" title="Polarity (international relations)">polarity</a> is not actually a major factor in the occurrence of wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 147â48">:â147â48â</span></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Power_transition_theory" title="Power transition theory">Power transition theory</a>: Hegemons impose stabilizing conditions on the world order, but they eventually decline, and war occurs when a declining hegemon is challenged by another rising power or aims to preemptively suppress them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-4"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">:â148â</span></sup> On this view, unlike for balance-of-power theory, wars become <i>more</i> probable when power is more equally distributed. This "power preponderance" hypothesis has empirical support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-5"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">:â148â</span></sup></li></ol>
<p>The two theories are not mutually exclusive and may be used to explain disparate events according to the circumstance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-6"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 148">:â148â</span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_(international_relations)" title="Liberalism (international relations)">Liberalism</a> as it relates to international relations emphasizes factors such as trade, and its role in disincentivizing conflict which will damage economic relations.  Realists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> respond that military force may sometimes be at least as effective as trade at achieving economic benefits, especially historically if not as much today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-7"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 149">:â149â</span></sup> Furthermore, trade relations which result in a high level of dependency may escalate tensions and lead to conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-8"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 150">:â150â</span></sup> Empirical data on the relationship of trade to peace are mixed, and moreover, some evidence suggests countries at war don't necessarily trade less with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-9"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 150">:â150â</span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal-level">Societal-level</span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Diversionary_foreign_policy" title="Diversionary foreign policy">Diversionary theory</a>, also known as the "scapegoat hypothesis", suggests the politically powerful may use war to as a diversion or to rally domestic popular support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-10"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 152">:â152â</span></sup> This is supported by literature showing out-group hostility <a href="/wiki/Realistic_conflict_theory" title="Realistic conflict theory">enhances</a> in-group <a href="/wiki/Group_cohesiveness" title="Group cohesiveness">bonding</a>, and a significant domestic "rally effect" has been demonstrated when conflicts begin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-11"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 152â13">:â152â13â</span></sup> However, studies examining the increased use of force as a function of need for internal political support are more mixed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-12"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 152â53">:â152â53â</span></sup> U.S. war-time presidential popularity surveys taken during the presidencies of several recent U.S. leaders have supported diversionary theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MilitaryAdventurism_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-MilitaryAdventurism-128">[127]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual-level">Individual-level</span></h4>
<p>These theories suggest differences in people's personalities, decision-making, emotions, belief systems, and biases are important in determining whether conflicts get out of hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-13"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 157">:â157â</span></sup> For instance, it has been proposed that conflict is modulated by <a href="/wiki/Bounded_rationality" title="Bounded rationality">bounded rationality</a> and various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_biases" title="Cognitive biases">cognitive biases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1998_127-14"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 157">:â157â</span></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Prospect_theory" title="Prospect theory">prospect theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levy1997_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1997-129">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/220px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/330px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/440px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Morning after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/John_Heaviside_Clark" title="John Heaviside Clark">John Heaviside Clark</a>, 1816</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> of war has been the subject of debate for thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeForrest_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeForrest-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The two principal aspects of ethics in war, according to the <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">just war theory</a>, are <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_ad_bellum" title="Jus ad bellum">jus ad bellum</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jus_in_bello" title="Jus in bello">jus in bello</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Jus ad bellum</i> (right to war), dictates which unfriendly acts and circumstances justify a proper authority in declaring war on another nation. There are six main criteria for the declaration of a just war: first, any just war must be declared by a lawful authority; second, it must be a just and righteous cause, with sufficient gravity to merit large-scale violence; third, the just belligerent must have rightful intentions â namely, that they seek to advance good and curtail evil; fourth, a just belligerent must have a reasonable chance of success; fifth, the war must be a last resort; and sixth, the ends being sought must be proportional to means being used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jus_in_bello" title="Jus in bello">Jus in bello</a></i> (right in war), is the set of ethical rules when conducting war. The two main principles are proportionality and discrimination. Proportionality regards how much force is necessary and morally appropriate to the ends being sought and the injustice suffered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moseley_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moseley-134">[133]</a></sup> The principle of discrimination determines who are the legitimate targets in a war, and specifically makes a separation between combatants, who it is permissible to kill, and non-combatants, who it is not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moseley_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moseley-134">[133]</a></sup> Failure to follow these rules can result in the loss of legitimacy for the just-war-belligerent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Codevilla,_Seabury_1989_304_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Codevilla,_Seabury_1989_304-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In besieged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leningrad" title="Leningrad">Leningrad</a>. "Hitler ordered that Moscow and Leningrad were to be razed to the ground; their inhabitants were to be annihilated or driven out by starvation. These intentions were part of the '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Plan_East" title="General Plan East">General Plan East</a>'."  â <i>The Oxford Companion to World War II.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup></div></div></div><p> The just war theory was foundational in the creation of the United Nations and in <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>'s regulations on legitimate war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeForrest_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeForrest-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">Fascism</a>, and the ideals it encompasses, such as <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">Pragmatism</a>, racism, and <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinism</a>, hold that violence is good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Pragmatism holds that war and violence can be good if it serves the ends of the people, without regard for universal morality. Racism holds that violence is good so that a master race can be established, or to purge an inferior race from the earth, or both. Social Darwinism asserts that violence is sometimes necessary to weed the unfit from society so civilization can flourish. These are broad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archetypes" title="Archetypes">archetypes</a> for the general position that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_ends_justify_the_means" title="The ends justify the means">the ends justify the means</a>. Lewis Coser, U.S. conflict theorist and sociologist, argued conflict provides a function and a process whereby a succession of new equilibriums are created. Thus, the struggle of opposing forces, rather than being disruptive, may be a means of balancing and maintaining a social structure or society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Limiting_and_stopping">Limiting and stopping</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg/220px-Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg/330px-Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg/440px-Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anti-war rally in Washington, D.C., 15 March 2003</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-war_movement" title="Anti-war movement">Anti-war movement</a></div><p>Religious groups have long formally opposed or sought to limit war as in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> document <i>Gaudiem et Spes</i>: "Any act of war aimed indiscriminately at the destruction of entire cities of extensive areas along with their population is a crime against God and man himself. It merits unequivocal and unhesitating condemnation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Anti-war movements have existed for every major war in the 20th century, including, most prominently, <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. In the 21st century, worldwide anti-war movements occurred in response to the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001âpresent)">invasion of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq</a>. Protests <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_to_the_War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="Opposition to the War in Afghanistan (2001âpresent)">opposing the War in Afghanistan</a> occurred in Europe, Asia, and the United States.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pauses">Pauses</span></h2>
<p>During a war, brief pauses of violence may be called for, and further agreed toâ <a href="/wiki/Ceasefire" title="Ceasefire">ceasefire</a>, temporary cessation, humanitarian pauses and corridors, days of tranquility, de-confliction arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-141">[140]</a></sup> There a number of disadvantages, obstacles and hesitations against implementing such pauses such as a <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian_corridor" title="Humanitarian corridor">humanitarian corridor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> Pauses in conflict are also ill-advised; reasons such as "delay of defeat" and the "weakening of credibility".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-144">[143]</a></sup> Natural causes for a pause may include events such as the 2019 <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">coronavirus pandemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "armed conflict" is used instead of, or in addition to, the term "war" with the former being more general in scope. The <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> differentiates between international and non-international armed conflict in their definition, "International armed conflicts exist whenever there is resort to armed force between two or more States.... Non-international armed conflicts are protracted armed confrontations occurring between governmental armed forces and the forces of one or more armed groups, or between such groups arising on the territory of a State [party to the Geneva Conventions]. The armed confrontation must reach a minimum level of intensity and the parties involved in the conflict must show a minimum of organisation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
